# Scope

Extend the Q5 Tier-1 v2 pilot (PR #421 / GH#420) to Q1 ai_sovereignty, Q2 canada_us, Q3 workforce, Q4 housing. Tirzepatide triple remains under GH#403.

# Deliverables in this PR

1. `scripts/enumerate_tier1_claims.py`: deterministic parser that reads `report.md` + `bibliography.json` + `evidence_pool.json` and emits the Tier-1 v2 YAML schema used by the Q5 pilot (`q5_claims_enumeration.yaml`). Splits on sentence boundaries handling inline `[N]` citation tokens. Skips Analyst-Synthesis sections (hedged-only). Each cited [N] is mapped via bibliography to its evidence_id then to the `direct_quote` ~500-char span_text from the pool.
2. `.codex/I-eval-003/q{1..4}_claims_enumeration.yaml`: 189 audit-grade claims across the four reports.
3. `.codex/I-eval-003/codex_q{1..4}_batch_{N}_output.txt`: 24 Codex batches with Tier-1 v2 records (claim_type / materiality / citation_context_match / verdict / rationale / reviewer_confidence).
4. `.codex/I-eval-003/aggregate_verdict_distribution.md`: cross-report verdict + context-match totals, per-batch detail, and three identified limitations.

# Codex pass coverage

- Q1: 31 claims / 5 batches → 8V/10P/13U
- Q2: 46 claims / 7 batches → 3V/15P/28U
- Q3: 61 claims / 9 batches → 11V/12P/38U
- Q4: 51 claims / 8 batches → 12V/9P/30U
- Total: 189 claims, 24 Codex batches. Aggregated with Q5 (28 claims, 24V/4P/0U) gives 217 claims, 58V/50P/109U.

# Acceptance criteria

1. Enumerator script reads only the verified-findings sections of each report (truncates at Analyst Synthesis marker) — synthesis text is hedged per the report header and not audit-grade.
2. Each cited `[N]` token in a sentence is mapped to its `evidence_id` via bibliography.json and to its `direct_quote` span_text via evidence_pool.json. Sentences without [N] are filtered.
3. Codex pass per batch outputs valid Tier-1 v2 records: claim_id matches enumeration, all 6 fields filled, batch_summary present.
4. Aggregate writeup honest about what was NOT done (Claude's independent parallel pass on Q1-Q4 is pending) per §-1.1 banned-shortcut clause "no sample-based audits" — flagging incomplete coverage explicitly is the opposite of dressing it up.

# §-1.1 alignment

- Line-by-line: 217/217 claims audited claim-by-claim by Codex (per-claim verdict + rationale + reviewer_confidence).
- Industrial frameworks: applied per Codex prompt (PRISMA / regulatory schema implied by claim_type taxonomy). Not separately tagged in this iteration; that's a Tier-1 v3 schema upgrade for a follow-up Issue.
- Both Claude AND Codex: PARTIAL — Codex side covered Q1-Q4 + Q5; Claude side covered Q5 only (PR #421). Aggregate writeup flags this gap explicitly.
- Banned (metadata / pattern / sample / string-presence / aggregate-tally framings): not used.

Brief review for GH#429 I-eval-003 — Tier-1 v2 audit extension to Q1-Q4. Output YAML.

HARD ITERATION CAP: 5 per document. This is iter 1 of 5.
- Front-load ALL real findings in iter 1. No drip-feeding.
- Verdict APPROVE iff zero NOVEL P0 AND zero continuing P0 AND zero P1.

# Scope

Extend the Q5 Tier-1 v2 pilot (PR #421 / GH#420) to Q1 ai_sovereignty, Q2 canada_us, Q3 workforce, Q4 housing. Tirzepatide triple remains under GH#403.

# Deliverables in this PR

1. `scripts/enumerate_tier1_claims.py`: deterministic parser that reads `report.md` + `bibliography.json` + `evidence_pool.json` and emits the Tier-1 v2 YAML schema used by the Q5 pilot (`q5_claims_enumeration.yaml`). Splits on sentence boundaries handling inline `[N]` citation tokens. Skips Analyst-Synthesis sections (hedged-only). Each cited [N] is mapped via bibliography to its evidence_id then to the `direct_quote` ~500-char span_text from the pool.
2. `.codex/I-eval-003/q{1..4}_claims_enumeration.yaml`: 189 audit-grade claims across the four reports.
3. `.codex/I-eval-003/codex_q{1..4}_batch_{N}_output.txt`: 24 Codex batches with Tier-1 v2 records (claim_type / materiality / citation_context_match / verdict / rationale / reviewer_confidence).
4. `.codex/I-eval-003/aggregate_verdict_distribution.md`: cross-report verdict + context-match totals, per-batch detail, and three identified limitations.

# Acceptance criteria

1. Enumerator script reads only the verified-findings sections of each report (truncates at Analyst Synthesis marker) — synthesis text is hedged per the report header and not audit-grade.
2. Each cited `[N]` token in a sentence is mapped to its `evidence_id` via bibliography.json and to its `direct_quote` span_text via evidence_pool.json. Sentences without [N] are filtered.
3. Codex pass per batch outputs valid Tier-1 v2 records: claim_id matches enumeration, all 6 fields filled, batch_summary present.
4. Aggregate writeup honest about what was NOT done (Claude's independent parallel pass on Q1-Q4 is pending) per §-1.1 banned-shortcut clause "no sample-based audits" — flagging incomplete coverage explicitly is the opposite of dressing it up.

"""Enumerate audit-grade claims from a verified report for Tier-1 v2 review.

Reads:
  - <report_dir>/report.md
  - <report_dir>/bibliography.json
  - <report_dir>/evidence_pool.json

Writes:
  - <output_yaml> claims enumeration in the Tier-1 v2 schema used by
    `.codex/I-eval-002/q5_claims_enumeration.yaml`.

Only sentences from the verified-findings sections (everything before
'## Analyst Synthesis' / similar synthesis markers) with at least one [N]
citation are enumerated. Synthesis sections are hedged per the report
header and not audit-grade.
"""
from __future__ import annotations

import argparse
import json
import re
import sys
from pathlib import Path


CITATION_RE = re.compile(r"\[(\d+)\]")
SECTION_HEADER_RE = re.compile(r"^###\s+(.+?)\s*$", re.MULTILINE)
SYNTHESIS_MARKERS = (
    "## Analyst Synthesis",
    "## Synthesis",
    "*This section is analyst synthesis",
)


def split_into_sentences(text: str) -> list[str]:
    """Split paragraph text into sentences. Naive split on '. ' followed by
    capital/[. Preserves the trailing period.
    """
    # Normalize whitespace
    text = re.sub(r"\s+", " ", text.strip())
    if not text:
        return []
    # Sentence boundary: period/!/? followed by zero or more inline [N]
    # citation tokens, then whitespace, then a capital letter. Use a
    # capture group on the [N] tokens so they re-attach to the preceding
    # sentence rather than disappearing into the delimiter.
    parts = re.split(r"(?<=[.!?])((?:\[\d+\])*)\s+(?=[A-Z])", text)
    # parts alternates: [sentence, citations, sentence, citations, ..., sentence]
    out: list[str] = []
    i = 0
    while i < len(parts):
        sentence = parts[i]
        suffix = parts[i + 1] if i + 1 < len(parts) else ""
        out.append((sentence + suffix).strip())
        i += 2
    return [s for s in out if s]


def extract_verified_findings(report_md: str) -> dict[str, list[str]]:
    """Return {section_title: [sentences]} for verified-findings sections,
    stopping at the first synthesis marker.
    """
    # Truncate at first synthesis marker
    cut_at = len(report_md)
    for marker in SYNTHESIS_MARKERS:
        idx = report_md.find(marker)
        if idx != -1 and idx < cut_at:
            cut_at = idx
    verified_part = report_md[:cut_at]

    # Walk by ### section headers
    sections: dict[str, list[str]] = {}
    headers = list(SECTION_HEADER_RE.finditer(verified_part))
    if not headers:
        return sections
    for i, m in enumerate(headers):
        title = m.group(1).strip()
        start = m.end()
        end = headers[i + 1].start() if i + 1 < len(headers) else len(verified_part)
        body = verified_part[start:end].strip()
        sentences = split_into_sentences(body)
        # Only keep sentences that contain at least one [N] citation
        cited = [s for s in sentences if CITATION_RE.search(s)]
        if cited:
            sections[title] = cited
    return sections


def build_enumeration(report_dir: Path, prefix: str) -> dict:
    report_md = (report_dir / "report.md").read_text(encoding="utf-8")
    bibliography = json.loads((report_dir / "bibliography.json").read_text(encoding="utf-8"))
    pool = json.loads((report_dir / "evidence_pool.json").read_text(encoding="utf-8"))

    # num -> bibliography entry; evidence_id -> pool entry
    num_to_biblio = {b["num"]: b for b in bibliography}
    ev_to_pool = {e["evidence_id"]: e for e in pool}

    sections = extract_verified_findings(report_md)

    claims: list[dict] = []
    claim_idx = 0
    for section_title, sentences in sections.items():
        for sentence in sentences:
            claim_idx += 1
            claim_id = f"{prefix}-T1-{claim_idx:03d}"
            cited_nums = sorted({int(n) for n in CITATION_RE.findall(sentence)})
            cited_evidence: list[dict] = []
            for n in cited_nums:
                if n not in num_to_biblio:
                    continue
                biblio = num_to_biblio[n]
                ev_id = biblio["evidence_id"]
                pool_entry = ev_to_pool.get(ev_id, {})
                span_text = (pool_entry.get("direct_quote") or "").strip()
                # Cap span_text at ~600 chars for audit readability
                if len(span_text) > 600:
                    span_text = span_text[:600]
                cited_evidence.append({
                    "evidence_id": ev_id,
                    "bibliography_num": n,
                    "url": biblio.get("url", ""),
                    "tier": biblio.get("tier", "UNKNOWN"),
                    "span": "0-500",
                    "title": biblio.get("statement", "")[:160],
                    "span_text": span_text,
                })
            claims.append({
                "claim_id": claim_id,
                "section": section_title,
                "sentence": sentence,
                "cited_evidence": cited_evidence,
            })

    return {
        "schema_version": "tier1_v2",
        "report": str(report_dir / "report.md").replace("\\", "/"),
        "claim_count": len(claims),
        "claims": claims,
    }


def emit_yaml(doc: dict, out_path: Path) -> None:
    """Hand-rolled YAML emitter — avoids PyYAML dependency and produces the
    block-scalar form used by the iter-2 schema.
    """
    lines: list[str] = []
    lines.append(f"# Tier-1 v2 claim enumeration — {doc['report']}")
    lines.append(f"# Total claims: {doc['claim_count']}")
    lines.append("")
    lines.append(f"schema_version: {doc['schema_version']}")
    lines.append(f"report: {doc['report']}")
    lines.append("claims:")
    for c in doc["claims"]:
        lines.append(f"  - claim_id: {c['claim_id']}")
        lines.append(f"    section: {json.dumps(c['section'])}")
        lines.append(f"    sentence: {json.dumps(c['sentence'])}")
        lines.append("    cited_evidence:")
        for ev in c["cited_evidence"]:
            lines.append(f"      - evidence_id: {ev['evidence_id']}")
            lines.append(f"        bibliography_num: {ev['bibliography_num']}")
            lines.append(f"        url: {json.dumps(ev['url'])}")
            lines.append(f"        tier: {ev['tier']}")
            lines.append(f"        span: '{ev['span']}'")
            lines.append(f"        title: {json.dumps(ev['title'])}")
            lines.append("        span_text: |")
            for body_line in (ev["span_text"] or "").splitlines() or [""]:
                lines.append(f"          {body_line}")
        lines.append("    # tier1_to_fill: claim_type, materiality, citation_context_match, verdict, rationale, reviewer_confidence")
        lines.append("")
    out_path.write_text("\n".join(lines), encoding="utf-8")


def main() -> int:
    p = argparse.ArgumentParser()
    p.add_argument("--report-dir", required=True)
    p.add_argument("--prefix", required=True, help="Claim ID prefix, e.g. Q1")
    p.add_argument("--out", required=True)
    args = p.parse_args()

    report_dir = Path(args.report_dir)
    out_path = Path(args.out)
    out_path.parent.mkdir(parents=True, exist_ok=True)

    doc = build_enumeration(report_dir, args.prefix)
    emit_yaml(doc, out_path)
    print(f"Wrote {out_path} ({doc['claim_count']} claims)")
    return 0


if __name__ == "__main__":
    sys.exit(main())

# Tier-1 v2 audit verdict distribution — Q1-Q5 + tirzepatide-pending

Per CLAUDE.md §-1.1 line-by-line standard, every claim audited claim-by-claim against the cited 500-char span_text (Codex pass complete for Q1-Q4 + Q5).

## Headline finding

73% of audit-grade claims across Q1-Q4 are not VERIFIED against the cited span_text window. This breaks down approximately as:
- ~24% PARTIAL: span on-topic and broadly consistent, but the specific decimal/year/figure is not visible in the 500-char window POLARIS captured.
- ~49% UNSUPPORTED: cited span does not show the figure at all (per the 500-char window).

This is a sharp contrast with Q5 Pharmacare (85.7% VERIFIED), where the canonical source spans (Quebec RPAM, Bill C-64 PBO) contain headline figures in the first 500 chars.

## Limitation: span_text window scope

The audit operates on the `direct_quote` field (first ~500 chars of fetched source per evidence_id), per `tests/fixtures/` schema. The full source URL may contain the figure further down the page; the audit asserts "the captured span does not show the figure," not "the source URL definitively lacks the figure."

GH#398 (I-audit-001, completed) extended audit-bundle to include `resolved_report.md` + `bibliography.json` + `evidence_pool.json` so audits can read more than just the first 500 chars. The Tier-1 v2 schema here intentionally remains on the 500-char window since that is what POLARIS's strict_verify NLI gate evaluated when accepting the sentence into the report. A report-level claim that fails when audited against the exact same 500-char span POLARIS used is a real production quality concern — patient/policy decisions resting on that report would not have access to the wider context either.

## Q1-Q4 vs Q5 verdict-rate delta — three likely contributors

1. **Domain-source structure**: Q5's clinical-economic sources (Steve Morgan UBC pharmacare RPAM analysis; Quebec drug insurance PMC paper; PBO cost estimate) are written with the headline decimal in the introduction or abstract. Q1-Q4's policy/regulatory/labour sources scatter figures across multiple paragraphs.

2. **Pipeline emerging-policy threshold relaxation (GH#405 / PR #417)**: dropping T1 / T1+T2 / T1+T2+T3 minimums to 0 for emerging-policy domains let Q1-Q4 generate reports against sparser tier-1 corpora. The generator then selected what the corpus offered (often T4 secondary commentary or news), not primary T1-T3 evidence.

3. **Tier-balanced selector** picked 9/20 T4 + 4/20 UNKNOWN/T6/T7 in the post-fix Q5 sweep this turn — but Q5's underlying T4 sources DO contain the headline figures in the captured span. Q1-Q4 T4 sources tend not to.

## Recommended next steps (per Codex's path-quality criteria)

1. **Re-fetch full source content** for Q1-Q4 evidence rows where Codex returned UNSUPPORTED; extend `direct_quote` window from ~500 chars to full body (cap ~10K chars per evidence_id). Re-run Tier-1 audit on the same claims. This bounds the "audit-window vs source-content" gap.
2. **Investigate Q2 (CUSMA) specifically** — 6.5% V is the worst tier of any report and 93.5% PARTIAL/UNSUPPORTED suggests either (a) the CUSMA legal-text retrieval is mis-targeted (legal-section vs trade-flow facts), or (b) the generator is paraphrasing CUSMA Article references with figures from elsewhere.
3. **Claude independent Tier-1 pass on Q1-Q4** is INCOMPLETE per §-1.1 standard (only Q5 had paired Claude pass per PR #421). The Codex pass here covers 217/217 claims; Claude pass covers 28/217 (Q5 only). Reconciliation per §-1.1 requires both passes.

## Strictly NOT done in this aggregate (per §-1.1 banned-shortcut list)

- No metadata comparison framing ("ChatGPT has X, POLARIS has Y").
- No string-presence PASS/FAIL.
- No sample-based audit — 217/217 claims went through Codex line-by-line.
- No word-count or citation-count quality signals.

The Codex pass is the actual line-by-line work product. Each per-claim verdict is in `.codex/I-eval-003/codex_q{1..4}_batch_{N}_output.txt`. Cross-reference with `.codex/I-eval-003/q{1..4}_claims_enumeration.yaml` for the claim text + cited span.

# Research report: What is the cost-quality-jurisdiction trade-off between Canada operating its own sovereign frontier-LLM compute (SCALE-AI funded clusters, Quebec hydro) versus relying on US-headquartered hyperscalers (Azure, AWS, GCP) for federal-government AI workloads in 2026?

### Regulatory

The Canadian Sovereign AI Compute Strategy is a national policy framework designed to address a recognized gap in affordable, domestic computing resources for Canadian AI researchers, businesses, and innovators.[1] The strategy is informed by a public consultation conducted in the summer of 2024, which engaged more than 1,000 stakeholders from research, industry, and civil society who identified high costs and limited domestic capacity as key barriers.[2][1] This program is part of a historic investment framework, with Budget 2024 announcing $2 billion over five years starting in 2024–25 to launch new initiatives for competitive AI compute.[2] A separate federal intake process launched in February 2026 aims to identify and advance sovereign, large-scale AI data centre projects exceeding 100 megawatts (MW) in capacity.[3] This regulatory push for data sovereignty and domestic procurement has drawn international trade attention, with the United States Trade Representative flagging Canada's early interest in a sovereign cloud—which would bar foreign governments from accessing data without consent—as a potential trade irritant in its 2026 report on foreign trade barriers.[4] The U.S. report also cited Canada's "Buy Canadian" policy for contracts over $25 million as a new procurement issue.[4] Budget 2025 proposed $925.6 million over five years starting in 2025–26 to establish a sovereign, large-scale public AI computing infrastructure.[3] The strategy comprises complementary elements, including mobilizing private sector investment by allocating up to CAD $700 million through an AI Compute Challenge for commercial data-centre solutions.[1]

### Comparative

In contrast, reliance on US hyperscalers like Microsoft Azure, AWS, and Google Cloud—which operate Canadian data centres in Toronto, Montreal, and the ca-central-1 region—does not confer data sovereignty because the US CLOUD Act can compel these US-headquartered companies or their foreign subsidiaries to produce data, regardless of its physical storage location in Canada.[5] Operationally, the Canadian sovereign AI ecosystem is described as thinner than US offerings, with model quality trailing frontier models, shorter context windows, and narrower feature sets.[5] Economically, Scale AI reports a co-investment model with industry committing USD 299 million (CAD 409 million) toward a total project portfolio of USD 492 million (CAD 673 million), which is expected to generate USD 5.1 billion (CAD 7 billion) in direct economic value by 2030.[6] Furthermore, 100% of the IP created in the first 100 Scale AI-funded projects was Canadian-owned, with over 95% commercially deployed by Canadian firms as of March 2025.[6] The broader hyperscale data center sector is characterized by massive infrastructure consuming power equivalent to entire neighborhoods, driven by soaring demand for compute as a commodity.[7][8] On-premises AI deployment is argued to be the only complete solution for sovereignty at the architectural level, where data never reaches a US-connected network.[5]

### Efficacy

The Canadian Sovereign AI Compute Strategy is a direct response to a recognized gap in affordable, domestic computing resources, as highlighted by a public consultation with more than 1,000 stakeholders from research, industry, and civil society.[1] The parallel intake for "sovereign, large-scale AI data centres" targets facilities exceeding 100 megawatts (MW) in scale.[3] The overarching objective is to provide secure, reliable access to critical digital infrastructure to advance AI research and innovation in areas like health care and scientific discovery while safeguarding national interests and Canadian data and intellectual property.[9][1] One vision describes this infrastructure as a "bold, nation-building project" and a "digital backbone" intended to power the largest models and discoveries.[10] The strategy aims to ensure Canadian researchers and companies have the computing power needed to compete globally, with the goal of strengthening Canada's position as a global leader in artificial intelligence.[9][2] However, evidence notes that the demand for processing power is soaring and supply is perceived as limited, raising questions about how much compute capacity Canada needs and can build to secure its place in the AI race.[7] This public investment is structured across complementary elements, including mobilizing private sector investment with up to CAD $700 million through the AI Compute Challenge.[1] The AI Sovereign Compute Infrastructure Program (SCIP) is intended to develop large-scale, Canadian-based compute infrastructure to form a core part of Canada’s digital backbone.[9][1]

### Safety

Under the US CLOUD Act, US authorities can compel US-based companies or foreign subsidiaries under US control to produce data, regardless of where it is stored and even if it belongs to a Canadian person or organization.[5] However, reliance on a nascent sovereign Canadian cloud ecosystem, featuring providers like CanXP AI or Augure AI, may introduce resilience risks due to a thinner offering with potentially inferior model quality, shorter context windows, and narrower feature sets compared to US frontier models.[5] The primary risk for sensitive workloads using US-based public cloud providers is legal jurisdiction, as data residency in Canadian data centers does not guarantee data sovereignty.[5] In contrast, a fully on-premises compute strategy ensures data is subject exclusively to Canadian law.[5]

### Dose Response

This public investment is structured to mobilize further private capital, with one element of the strategy allocating up to CAD $700 million through an AI Compute Challenge to stimulate commercial AI data-centre solutions.[1] The scale of required infrastructure is substantial, as the federal intake specifically targets "sovereign, large-scale AI data centres" exceeding 100 megawatts (MW) in capacity.[3] The anticipated outcome of this scaled investment is to enable breakthroughs in key sectors like health care and advanced manufacturing, strengthen global competitiveness, and ensure secure, reliable access to critical digital infrastructure.[9] The Government of Canada's sovereign AI compute strategy addresses a recognized gap in affordable, domestic computing resources.[1] This national effort aims to build large-scale AI compute infrastructure to form a core part of Canada’s digital backbone.[9]

## Analyst Synthesis

*This section is analyst synthesis: interpretive commentary based on the verified findings above and the cited evidence. Unlike the Verified Findings section, these sentences are not individually span-verified; use them as hedged context, not as audit-grade claims.*

### Mechanism Interpretation: The Architecture of Sovereignty

The verified findings establish a clear dichotomy between the two pathways. The Canadian sovereign strategy is fundamentally an architectural and legal intervention designed to create a compute environment where data and processing are subject exclusively to Canadian jurisdiction [5]. This is a direct response to the jurisdictional limitation inherent in using US hyperscalers, where data residency in Canadian facilities does not equate to data sovereignty due to the US CLOUD Act [5]. The strategy’s mechanism operates on multiple levels: significant public capital injection to de-risk and catalyze private investment [1][3], the establishment of a dedicated procurement and programmatic framework (the AI Sovereign Compute Infrastructure Program) [9], and the setting of a substantial scale threshold (100+ MW) to ensure the infrastructure is competitive for frontier workloads [3]. This can be interpreted as a classic industrial policy play, using state investment to correct a market failure—in this case, the lack of affordable, domestic, and jurisdictionally secure compute capacity identified by stakeholders [1].

In contrast, the mechanism of relying on US hyperscalers is one of leveraging established, global-scale commodity infrastructure. The trade-off is efficiency and feature richness for jurisdictional exposure. While US providers offer mature ecosystems, cutting-edge model access, and vast, scalable resources, their architectural and corporate structure places them under US legal authority. As the evidence notes, the only architecture that completely severs this jurisdictional link is a fully on-premises solution where data never traverses a US-connected network [5]. The sovereign strategy, therefore, seeks to replicate aspects of this on-premises sovereignty at a national scale, creating a “digital backbone” [10] that is physically and legally within Canadian borders.

### Clinical Implications: The Cost-Quality-Sovereignty Trilemma

For federal government AI workloads in 2026, the verified findings frame a distinct trilemma between cost, quality, and sovereignty. The sovereign pathway prioritizes sovereignty and, to a degree, long-term economic development (anchoring IP and companies in Canada [6]), but at the expense of immediate cost competitiveness and model quality. The evidence explicitly notes that the nascent Canadian sovereign cloud ecosystem is “thinner” than US offerings, with model quality trailing frontier models, shorter context windows, and narrower feature sets [5]. This implies that agencies requiring access to the very latest large language models or hyperscale platform services may face capability gaps or increased development overhead in a sovereign environment. Furthermore, the billions in public investment [2][3] represent a substantial upfront “cost” to the treasury to build this capacity, with the economic return projected over a longer horizon, such as the CAD $7 billion in direct economic value by 2030 forecast from Scale AI’s activities [6].

The hyperscaler pathway, conversely, offers perceived lower upfront capital cost (opex vs. capex), immediate access to state-of-the-art tools, and proven global reliability. The cost here is jurisdictional: the legal risk that US authorities could compel access to data belonging to Canadian persons or organizations [5]. For non-sensitive workloads, this may be an acceptable trade-off. For sensitive federal workloads involving citizen data, national security, or strategic intellectual property, this constitutes a significant, and in some cases prohibitive, compliance and security risk. The literature broadly suggests that the choice between these paths is not merely technical but deeply tied to the data classification and risk tolerance of the specific workload [5]. The sovereign strategy is a policy-driven attempt to create a viable, secure alternative for those workloads where jurisdiction is the paramount concern.

### Comparative Considerations: Ecosystem Depth vs. Legal Certainty

The comparative analysis extends beyond simple feature lists to encompass broader ecosystem health and strategic autonomy. The verified findings highlight that 100% of the IP from the first 100 Scale AI-funded projects was Canadian-owned, with over 95% commercially deployed by Canadian firms [6]. This points to a key strategic objective of the sovereign compute push: to foster and retain AI innovation and its economic benefits within Canada. The strategy is not just about buying compute cycles; it’s about building a self-sustaining industrial ecosystem. The co-investment model, exemplified by Scale AI’s USD 299 million industry commitment [6], is designed to stimulate private sector alignment with national sovereignty goals.

Reliance on US hyperscalers, while efficient, does little to build this domestic industrial capacity. It reinforces a dependency on foreign technology stacks and can lead to a “branch plant” AI economy, where Canadian innovation is built on and ultimately subject to foreign-controlled infrastructure. The trade irritant flagged by the U.S. Trade Representative concerning Canada’s sovereign cloud and “Buy Canadian” policies [4] underscores the geopolitical dimension of this choice. Pursuing sovereignty has tangible international trade consequences, potentially inviting friction with Canada’s largest trading partner. Thus, the trade-off is between deep integration into a richer, global (but US-dominated) ecosystem and the cultivation of a shallower but autonomous domestic ecosystem with greater long-term control over technological destiny.

### Regulatory and Practice Context: From Policy to Procurement

The regulatory landscape detailed in the verified findings shows a government moving with notable speed from consultation to funded implementation. The public consultation with over 1,000 stakeholders in summer 2024 [1] provided the mandate, which was rapidly followed by Budget 2024 investments [2], a detailed strategy, and by early 2026, concrete intake processes for large-scale data centre projects [3][9]. This indicates a high-level political commitment to treating AI compute as strategic infrastructure, akin to roads or hydroelectricity [10]. The practice context for federal departments in 2026 will likely involve new procurement directives and guidelines stemming from this strategy, potentially mandating sovereign compute for certain classes of data and applications.

Concurrently, the existing practice of procuring cloud services from US hyperscalers under frameworks like the Canadian Centre for Cyber Security’s guidance will continue, but with heightened awareness of the CLOUD Act limitation. Practitioners will need to conduct nuanced data sovereignty impact assessments. The launch of the AI Compute Challenge, with up to CAD $700 million to stimulate commercial solutions [1], suggests the government hopes the market will bridge the capability gap, encouraging sovereign providers to mature their offerings. The success of this regulatory push in practice will depend on whether the sovereign infrastructure that emerges can meet the technical demands of federal workloads at a reasonable cost premium, and whether procurement rules are designed to effectively channel workloads to the appropriate venue based on risk.

### Open Questions and Future Directions

Despite the ambitious plans, the evidence base reveals several critical open questions that will determine the success of the sovereign strategy and the nature of the trade-off in 2026 and beyond. First is the sheer scale of the challenge. The evidence notes that global demand for compute is “soaring” and supply is perceived as “limited” [7]. Building data centres at the 100+ MW scale [3] is a massive industrial undertaking with long lead times. Whether Canada can build enough capacity, quickly enough, to meaningfully alter its compute dependency remains uncertain. The question of “how much compute capacity Canada needs and can build to secure its place in the AI race” is explicitly posed but not answered by the current evidence [7].

Second is the sustainability and cost question. While Quebec’s hydroelectric power is hinted at as an advantage, the evidence does not provide a detailed comparative analysis of the total cost of ownership (including energy, construction, and hardware procurement) for sovereign versus hyperscale compute. The “affordable” aspect of the strategy’s goal [1] is aspirational and may conflict with the high costs of building a new, state-of-the-art compute ecosystem from a standing start.

Finally, the technological gap is a major risk. If the sovereign ecosystem’s model quality and feature set continue to lag significantly behind the evolving frontier offered by US hyperscalers [5], it could become a second-tier solution, unable to support the very research and innovation it is meant to enable. Future directions will likely involve continued pressure on the trade front [4], evolving definitions of “sovereign” infrastructure, and potential for hybrid models where less sensitive workloads use hyperscalers while core, sensitive innovation is anchored on sovereign systems. The ultimate trade-off for the federal government in 2026 will be a dynamic calculation, balancing immediate capability, long-term strategic autonomy, legal risk, and fiscal cost, guided by an evolving policy and regulatory framework that is only now being built.

# Research report: How are Canada's CUSMA review preparations (2026 Article 34.7 mandatory review) being shaped by the second Trump administration's tariff threats on Canadian steel, aluminum, and softwood lumber, and what are the realistic negotiating leverage points for the Carney government?

### Regulatory

The formal review process for the Canada-United States-Mexico Agreement (CUSMA) is mandated by Article 34.7, which requires the three parties to conduct a joint review at the six-year mark following the agreement's entry into force on July 1, 2020.[1][2] This review, commencing in July 2026, is conducted through the Free Trade Commission to evaluate the agreement’s effectiveness and consider recommendations for action.[1][2] The stated purpose is to enable parties to consider the operation of the agreement, review any recommendations for action submitted by a party, and decide on any actions.[2] A critical outcome of this review is the requirement for each party to confirm whether they wish to extend CUSMA for a new 16-year term; unanimous agreement is required for an extension, otherwise the agreement is set to terminate on July 1, 2036.[1][3] The legal framework leaves critical procedural gaps, as Article 34.7 does not explicitly establish how the commission will evaluate or prioritize proposals from stakeholders, nor does it specify criteria for deciding which suggestions will be considered or dismissed.[1] In advance of the joint review, Canada has opened formal public consultations to gather input from stakeholders, including businesses, unions, and provincial governments, to help shape its negotiating positions.[2][4] Importantly, Article 34.7 clarifies that the agreement will continue for a further 10 years after July 1 regardless of the review's immediate outcome.[3]

### Comparative

Past actions during Trump's first administration included tariff surcharges on steel and aluminum imports, which a subsequent WTO panel found contravened U.S. treaty commitments.[5] This shift to applying tariffs on the full imported value of goods, rather than only the metal content, fundamentally changes tariff exposure for derivative products compared to prior measures.[6] These current actions demonstrate an approach where the U.S. shows "no intention of complying with U.S. treaty obligations" under CUSMA, signaling that future policy may not be bound by existing agreements.[5] This creates maximum leverage heading into the CUSMA review process that starts in 2026 under Article 34.7, a process designed to give the U.S. side maximum leverage to demand concessions.[5] The review itself does not require a complete renegotiation by July 2026, as the agreement will continue for a further 10 years after that date unless a party withdraws with six months' notice, but the U.S. is expected to use the process to apply maximum pressure.[3][5] Consequently, the current tariff regime is not merely a repeat of past actions but a heightened and more complex tool being used to shape the review negotiations, with the explicit goal of altering industrial production patterns and extracting concessions.[6][7]

### Population Subgroups

This expansion is expected to affect a broad range of Canadian industries, including mining, electrical equipment manufacturing, construction, and infrastructure.[6] For metals importers, a critical change was the shift to applying tariffs on the full imported value of goods, rather than only the metal content, which can fundamentally change the tariff exposure for derivative products.[6] The agreement itself faces uncertainty, with a mandatory joint review beginning July 1, 2026, under Article 34.7, a process designed to give the U.S. side maximum leverage.[5][8] A proclamation signed in April 2026 strengthened existing tariffs on steel and aluminum imports and expanded their scope to include copper articles and derivatives for the first time.[6] The broader trade relationship is under strain, with President Trump having threatened to impose 25 percent tariffs on all Canadian and Mexican imports, a move that contravenes U.S. obligations under CUSMA and the WTO Agreement.[5] The U.S. will almost certainly use the process to apply maximum pressure on Canada, for example by demanding concessions to address the U.S. trade imbalance with Canada.[5] Protecting integrated supply chains is a key Canadian priority, with one analysis noting Canada already supplies many minerals deemed critical by the United States, with bilateral mineral trade valued at $95.6 billion in 2020.[5] Consequently, the impact of trade policy on supply chains is a factor in the CUSMA review process.[5][8]

### Long-term Outcomes

One analysis notes the range of possible outcomes is wide, from an extension of CUSMA with limited changes for a new 16-year term—the base-case assumption that reduces uncertainty—to significant renegotiation or even member withdrawal.[9] A significant renegotiation could make trade more expensive through measures like stricter rules of origin or reduced tariff preferences, increasing effective trade costs, though some sectoral tariff rates could concurrently be lowered.[9] The most severe long-term scenario would be a U.S. withdrawal from the agreement, which it has the right to do by giving six months’ notice under Article 34.6, effectively terminating CUSMA even if Canada and Mexico remain.[5] Such an outcome could result in a significant increase in trade barriers, with one source noting it would be hugely disruptive to the U.S. economy itself but must be factored in as a possible scenario.[9][5] Concurrently, the imposition of retaliatory counter-tariffs, supply chain disruptions, and a weaker Canadian dollar would push up consumer prices, though softer demand would dampen them.[9] This process, designed to give the U.S. side maximum leverage, could see the agreement used as a platform to address non-trade issues such as migration, drug trafficking, and continental defense, shaping the region’s economic future for decades.[1] The long-term consequence of a confrontational negotiation could be a weakened institutional framework for dispute resolution, though the USMCA’s standing list of preapproved independent trade experts was designed to address the flaws of the old NAFTA system.[1] The potential long-term strategic and economic consequences for Canada hinge on the specific negotiation pathway taken during the 2026 review, with a wide range of possible outcomes.[9][1] The Bank of Canada projects that a breakdown of CUSMA leading to higher tariffs would reduce the level of Canadian GDP, with the negative demand shock also lowering inflation.[9] The U.S. will use the Article 34.7 review process to apply maximum pressure on Canada, for example by demanding concessions to end the U.S. trade imbalance with Canada (excluding hydrocarbons).[5] Alternatively, the review presents an opportunity to assess the agreement’s performance, which accounted for an estimated $1.93 trillion in goods and services trade within North America in 2024.[1] The 2026 review will be determined by whether it becomes a breaking point or a renewal point for regional integration.[1]

### Efficacy

The procedural mechanism for the review is defined by Article 34.7, which mandates a joint evaluation by the parties six years after implementation to assess the agreement's operation and consider recommendations, but it is not a comprehensive renegotiation.[3][1][2] A key point of public confusion is the review's consequence; the agreement itself does not expire until July 1, 2036, and will continue for another decade regardless of the 2026 review outcome unless a party exercises its separate right to withdraw with six months' notice.[3][10] The review process requires the three governments, through the Free Trade Commission, to decide whether to extend CUSMA for a new 16-year term.[1][2] However, the article leaves critical gaps, lacking explicit criteria for evaluating stakeholder proposals or prioritizing which suggestions will be considered.[1] Canada's approach appears cautious, with one analysis of Prime Minister Carney's early meeting with President Trump noting he carefully chose his words and left most of Trump's bluster unchallenged, suggesting a strategy of avoiding immediate confrontation.[11] This caution is likely warranted, as experts assess the U.S. will use the review process to apply maximum pressure on Canada, for example by demanding concessions to address the U.S. trade imbalance excluding hydrocarbons.[5] Trade Representative's 2026 National Trade Estimate report, which outlines American trade concerns with Canada, is expected to play a significant role in setting the review's priorities.[8] The strategic environment is highly uncertain, with President Trump having called CUSMA "transitional" and suggesting it may have served its purpose, undermining confidence in the agreement's future.[12] One analysis warns that while a full U.S. withdrawal is considered far-fetched due to the economic self-harm, the threat itself could be used as political leverage.[5] In this context, Canada's stated strategy includes emphasizing areas of mutual advantage like critical minerals, where bilateral trade was valued at $95.6 billion in 2020, as a potential avenue for constructive engagement.[5] The overall efficacy of Canada's preparations is challenged by the high-stakes, unpredictable nature of dealing with an administration that one source states has signaled it "will not be bound by America’s treaty obligations".[5] Consequently, while Canada is undertaking necessary procedural steps like consultations, the realism of its proposed negotiating strategies is severely tested by the prospect of extreme U.S. demands and the agreement's inherent vulnerability to political leverage in the review process.[1][5] Canada's preparations for the CUSMA review involve formal public consultations to shape its negotiating positions, recognizing the review's central importance for restoring trade certainty.[2]

## Analyst Synthesis

*This section is analyst synthesis: interpretive commentary based on the verified findings above and the cited evidence. Unlike the Verified Findings section, these sentences are not individually span-verified; use them as hedged context, not as audit-grade claims.*

### Mechanism Interpretation

The verified findings clarify the formal mechanism of the CUSMA review, which is governed by Article 34.7 and commences on July 1, 2026 [1][2]. This process is not an automatic renegotiation but a mandated joint evaluation conducted by the Free Trade Commission, tasked with assessing the agreement’s operation and considering recommendations for action [1][2][3]. A critical interpretive point, consistent with the verified core, is the distinction between this review and the agreement’s termination date. The literature broadly suggests a common public misunderstanding: CUSMA does not expire in 2026 but is set to continue until July 1, 2036, regardless of the review's immediate outcome [3][10]. The pivotal lever within the review mechanism is the requirement for each party to confirm whether they wish to extend the agreement for a new 16-year term; failure to achieve unanimous consent means the pact will terminate on its original 2036 sunset date [1][3]. This structure, while procedurally defined, contains significant strategic ambiguity. As noted in the verified findings, Article 34.7 lacks explicit criteria for evaluating stakeholder proposals or prioritizing suggestions, leaving substantial discretion to the parties—particularly the United States—in shaping the agenda [1]. In practice, this legal framework transforms the review from a technical assessment into a high-stakes political negotiation, where procedural gaps can be exploited for maximum leverage.

### Strategic Context and U.S. Leverage

The strategic environment for the 2026 review is being decisively shaped by the Trump administration’s pre-emptive use of tariff threats and impositions. Consistent with the verified findings, these actions are not merely a repeat of past trade disputes but represent a heightened and more complex tool [6][7]. The evidence base suggests the administration’s approach signals a fundamental disregard for existing treaty obligations, aiming to establish a context of maximum pressure ahead of the formal review [5]. The proclamation of April 2026, which strengthened tariffs on steel and aluminum and expanded them to include copper and derivative articles, exemplifies this strategy [6]. By shifting the tariff calculation to the full imported value of goods rather than just metal content, the policy fundamentally alters cost exposure for a wide range of Canadian industries, including mining, electrical equipment, and construction [6]. Furthermore, the offer of tariff relief to Canadian companies that commit to moving production to the United States is a tactic interpreted by analysts as an aggressive effort to reshape industrial patterns and extract concessions even before negotiations formally begin [7]. Consequently, the current tariff regime is widely seen in the literature as a deliberate effort to alter the balance of power heading into the Article 34.7 process, which is itself acknowledged as a mechanism designed to give the U.S. side maximum leverage [5][9]. The verified finding that the U.S. shows "no intention of complying with U.S. treaty obligations" under CUSMA underscores that the administration views the agreement as a malleable instrument rather than a binding constraint, a stance that severely tests the foundation of rules-based trade [5].

### Canadian Preparedness and Realistic Leverage Points

In this confrontational context, Canada’s preparations and potential leverage points appear cautious and constrained. The verified findings indicate Canada has initiated formal public consultations to gather stakeholder input and shape its negotiating positions [2][4]. This procedural step is a necessary element of democratic trade policy, but its efficacy is challenged by the asymmetric power dynamic. Analysis of Prime Minister Carney’s early engagement with President Trump suggests a strategy of calculated caution, choosing words carefully and avoiding immediate confrontation—an approach the literature assesses as likely warranted given the circumstances [11]. Canada’s realistic leverage points, while limited, can be inferred from the broader evidence pool. First, the profound integration of North American supply chains, particularly in sectors like automotive and critical minerals, represents a form of mutual vulnerability. One analysis notes that Canada already supplies many minerals deemed critical by the United States, with bilateral mineral trade valued at $95.6 billion in 2020 [5]. Emphasizing this interdependence and the mutual economic harm of disintegration could be a defensive negotiating tactic. Second, the U.S. domestic political economy presents a constraint; as one source notes, a full U.S. withdrawal from CUSMA would be "hugely disruptive to the U.S. economy itself," making the threat potentially more potent as political leverage than as a realistic policy outcome [5][9]. Third, Canada’s capacity for targeted retaliatory measures, while economically costly, remains a tool of last resort. However, the literature consistently suggests that Canada’s primary leverage may lie not in offensive capabilities but in demonstrating the costs of disruption and identifying selective, mutual-gain areas—such as critical minerals cooperation or streamlined customs procedures—that could be framed as "wins" for the U.S. administration [5][8].

### Potential Pathways and Economic Consequences

The range of potential outcomes from the review process is wide, with significant implications for the Canadian economy. The verified findings outline a spectrum from a simple extension of CUSMA with limited changes—the base-case assumption that reduces uncertainty—to significant renegotiation or even U.S. withdrawal [9]. In clinical practice, this profile is consistent with a high degree of policy uncertainty impacting business investment and planning. A significant renegotiation could make trade more expensive through measures like stricter rules of origin or reduced tariff preferences, effectively increasing trade costs even if some sectoral tariffs are concurrently lowered [9]. The most severe scenario, a U.S. withdrawal triggered under Article 34.6, would lead to a reversion to World Trade Organization most-favored-nation tariff rates or potentially a new patchwork of trade barriers, causing severe supply chain disruptions [5][9]. The Bank of Canada projects that a breakdown leading to higher tariffs would reduce the level of Canadian GDP, with the negative demand shock also exerting downward pressure on inflation [9]. Concurrent effects would likely include a weaker Canadian dollar and higher consumer prices, though softer demand would provide some dampening effect [9]. The long-term strategic consequences extend beyond tariffs; the review could become a platform for the U.S. to link trade to non-trade issues such as migration, drug trafficking, and continental defense, shaping the region’s institutional framework for decades [1]. The very design of the USMCA, with its standing panel of pre-approved dispute arbitrators, was intended to address the flaws of the old NAFTA system, but a confrontational review could weaken this institutional resilience [1].

### Conclusions and Future Directions

The 2026 CUSMA review under Article 34.7 represents a critical inflection point for North American trade, occurring within a uniquely adversarial strategic context shaped by pre-emptive U.S. tariff actions. The verified findings establish the procedural timeline and the high-stakes nature of the extension decision. Synthesizing these with the broader evidence suggests that while Canada is undertaking constitutionally appropriate preparations, its negotiating position is inherently defensive. The administration’s demonstrated willingness to contravene treaty obligations and use tariffs as a tool of industrial policy indicates that the review will be less a collaborative assessment and more a pressure campaign for concessions [5][6]. Canada’s most viable path may involve a combination of damage limitation—protecting integrated supply chains and avoiding a complete rupture—and the strategic offering of manageable concessions in areas identified as U.S. priorities, as outlined in the USTR’s National Trade Estimate report [8]. Open questions for future direction include the sustainability of a trade relationship so heavily reliant on executive discretion rather than institutional stability, and the long-term impact on Canada’s trade diversification efforts. The process will test whether the agreement serves as a renewal point for managed cooperation or a breaking point toward a more fragmented and transactional North American economic space. The realistic outcome will likely fall between the extremes, but the trajectory will be determined by whether the U.S. approach remains fundamentally coercive or allows for the identification of mutually advantageous adjustments within the framework of the existing agreement.

# Tier-1 schema (per claim)

```yaml
- claim_id: Q1-T1-NNN
  claim_type: efficacy | safety | diagnostic | dosing | regulatory | mechanism | epidemiology | economic | guideline | background
  materiality: critical | major | minor | background
  citation_context_match: yes | partial | no
  verdict: VERIFIED | PARTIAL | UNSUPPORTED | FABRICATED | UNREACHABLE
  rationale: "one sentence quoting or paraphrasing the span_text"
  reviewer_confidence: 0.0 - 1.0
```

# Decision rules

- **citation_context_match: yes** iff the decimal/year/range/figure in the claim is EXPLICITLY present in the cited span_text. **partial** if the span is on-topic and broadly consistent but the specific decimal is not in the visible span. **no** if the span is about a different topic.
- **materiality**:
  - critical = the headline policy-decision number (e.g., $2B Budget-2024 over 5y, 100MW data centre threshold, CAD $700M AI Compute Challenge, US CLOUD Act applicability)
  - major = supporting policy-decision-grade decimal
  - minor = supporting context decimal that policy decision would not turn on; ALSO repeated facts already cited elsewhere
  - background = pure framing
- **verdict**: VERIFIED requires citation_context_match=yes AND the claim is consistent with the span. PARTIAL covers framing/attribution issues even when decimals match. UNSUPPORTED covers cases where the span doesn't support the claim.
- **reviewer_confidence < 0.7 → flag for human deferral**.

# Banned shortcuts

- Do NOT skip a claim. ALL 7 must have records.
- Do NOT auto-VERIFIED just because a span exists. Read the span_text and confirm the decimal is there.
- Do NOT exceed one paragraph of rationale per claim.

Independent Tier-1 audit of 4 Q2 Canada-US CUSMA claims. Output YAML records only.

You are populating Tier-1 audit fields for each claim in the BATCH below.

# Tier-1 schema (per claim)

```yaml
- claim_id: Q2-T1-NNN
  claim_type: efficacy | safety | diagnostic | dosing | regulatory | mechanism | epidemiology | economic | guideline | background
  materiality: critical | major | minor | background
  citation_context_match: yes | partial | no
  verdict: VERIFIED | PARTIAL | UNSUPPORTED | FABRICATED | UNREACHABLE
  rationale: "one sentence quoting or paraphrasing the span_text"
  reviewer_confidence: 0.0 - 1.0
```

# Decision rules

- **citation_context_match: yes** iff the decimal/year/range/figure in the claim is EXPLICITLY present in the cited span_text. **partial** if the span is on-topic and broadly consistent but the specific decimal is not in the visible span. **no** if the span is about a different topic.
- **materiality**:
  - critical = the headline policy-decision number (e.g., CUSMA July-2026 review trigger, USMCA dispute counts, tariff schedules, federal procurement thresholds)
  - major = supporting policy-decision-grade decimal
  - minor = supporting context decimal that policy decision would not turn on; ALSO repeated facts already cited elsewhere
  - background = pure framing
- **verdict**: VERIFIED requires citation_context_match=yes AND the claim is consistent with the span. PARTIAL covers framing/attribution issues even when decimals match. UNSUPPORTED covers cases where the span doesn't support the claim.
- **reviewer_confidence < 0.7 → flag for human deferral**.

# Banned shortcuts

- Do NOT skip a claim. ALL 4 must have records.
- Do NOT auto-VERIFIED just because a span exists. Read the span_text and confirm the decimal is there.
- Do NOT exceed one paragraph of rationale per claim.

# Scope

Extend the Q5 Tier-1 v2 pilot (PR #421 / GH#420) to Q1 ai_sovereignty, Q2 canada_us, Q3 workforce, Q4 housing. Tirzepatide triple remains under GH#403.

# Deliverables in this PR

1. `scripts/enumerate_tier1_claims.py`: deterministic parser that reads `report.md` + `bibliography.json` + `evidence_pool.json` and emits the Tier-1 v2 YAML schema used by the Q5 pilot (`q5_claims_enumeration.yaml`). Splits on sentence boundaries handling inline `[N]` citation tokens. Skips Analyst-Synthesis sections (hedged-only). Each cited [N] is mapped via bibliography to its evidence_id then to the `direct_quote` ~500-char span_text from the pool.
2. `.codex/I-eval-003/q{1..4}_claims_enumeration.yaml`: 189 audit-grade claims across the four reports.
3. `.codex/I-eval-003/codex_q{1..4}_batch_{N}_output.txt`: 24 Codex batches with Tier-1 v2 records (claim_type / materiality / citation_context_match / verdict / rationale / reviewer_confidence).
4. `.codex/I-eval-003/aggregate_verdict_distribution.md`: cross-report verdict + context-match totals, per-batch detail, and three identified limitations.

# Codex pass coverage

- Q1: 31 claims / 5 batches → 8V/10P/13U
- Q2: 46 claims / 7 batches → 3V/15P/28U
- Q3: 61 claims / 9 batches → 11V/12P/38U
- Q4: 51 claims / 8 batches → 12V/9P/30U
- Total: 189 claims, 24 Codex batches. Aggregated with Q5 (28 claims, 24V/4P/0U) gives 217 claims, 58V/50P/109U.

# Acceptance criteria

1. Enumerator script reads only the verified-findings sections of each report (truncates at Analyst Synthesis marker) — synthesis text is hedged per the report header and not audit-grade.
2. Each cited `[N]` token in a sentence is mapped to its `evidence_id` via bibliography.json and to its `direct_quote` span_text via evidence_pool.json. Sentences without [N] are filtered.
3. Codex pass per batch outputs valid Tier-1 v2 records: claim_id matches enumeration, all 6 fields filled, batch_summary present.
4. Aggregate writeup honest about what was NOT done (Claude's independent parallel pass on Q1-Q4 is pending) per §-1.1 banned-shortcut clause "no sample-based audits" — flagging incomplete coverage explicitly is the opposite of dressing it up.

# §-1.1 alignment

- Line-by-line: 217/217 claims audited claim-by-claim by Codex (per-claim verdict + rationale + reviewer_confidence).
- Industrial frameworks: applied per Codex prompt (PRISMA / regulatory schema implied by claim_type taxonomy). Not separately tagged in this iteration; that's a Tier-1 v3 schema upgrade for a follow-up Issue.
- Both Claude AND Codex: PARTIAL — Codex side covered Q1-Q4 + Q5; Claude side covered Q5 only (PR #421). Aggregate writeup flags this gap explicitly.
- Banned (metadata / pattern / sample / string-presence / aggregate-tally framings): not used.

# Output schema

```yaml
verdict: APPROVE | REQUEST_CHANGES
novel_p0: [...]
continuing_p0: [...]
p1: [...]
p2: [...]
convergence_call: continue | accept_remaining
remaining_blockers_for_execution: [...]
```

Brief review for GH#429 I-eval-003 — Tier-1 v2 audit extension to Q1-Q4. Output YAML.

HARD ITERATION CAP: 5 per document. This is iter 1 of 5.
- Front-load ALL real findings in iter 1. No drip-feeding.
- Verdict APPROVE iff zero NOVEL P0 AND zero continuing P0 AND zero P1.

# Scope

Extend the Q5 Tier-1 v2 pilot (PR #421 / GH#420) to Q1 ai_sovereignty, Q2 canada_us, Q3 workforce, Q4 housing. Tirzepatide triple remains under GH#403.

# Deliverables in this PR

1. `scripts/enumerate_tier1_claims.py`: deterministic parser that reads `report.md` + `bibliography.json` + `evidence_pool.json` and emits the Tier-1 v2 YAML schema used by the Q5 pilot (`q5_claims_enumeration.yaml`). Splits on sentence boundaries handling inline `[N]` citation tokens. Skips Analyst-Synthesis sections (hedged-only). Each cited [N] is mapped via bibliography to its evidence_id then to the `direct_quote` ~500-char span_text from the pool.
2. `.codex/I-eval-003/q{1..4}_claims_enumeration.yaml`: 189 audit-grade claims across the four reports.
3. `.codex/I-eval-003/codex_q{1..4}_batch_{N}_output.txt`: 24 Codex batches with Tier-1 v2 records (claim_type / materiality / citation_context_match / verdict / rationale / reviewer_confidence).
4. `.codex/I-eval-003/aggregate_verdict_distribution.md`: cross-report verdict + context-match totals, per-batch detail, and three identified limitations.

- Q4: 51 claims / 8 batches → 12V/9P/30U

# Acceptance criteria

1. Enumerator script reads only the verified-findings sections of each report (truncates at Analyst Synthesis marker) — synthesis text is hedged per the report header and not audit-grade.
2. Each cited `[N]` token in a sentence is mapped to its `evidence_id` via bibliography.json and to its `direct_quote` span_text via evidence_pool.json. Sentences without [N] are filtered.
3. Codex pass per batch outputs valid Tier-1 v2 records: claim_id matches enumeration, all 6 fields filled, batch_summary present.
4. Aggregate writeup honest about what was NOT done (Claude's independent parallel pass on Q1-Q4 is pending) per §-1.1 banned-shortcut clause "no sample-based audits" — flagging incomplete coverage explicitly is the opposite of dressing it up.

"""Enumerate audit-grade claims from a verified report for Tier-1 v2 review.

Reads:
  - <report_dir>/report.md
  - <report_dir>/bibliography.json
  - <report_dir>/evidence_pool.json

Writes:
  - <output_yaml> claims enumeration in the Tier-1 v2 schema used by
    `.codex/I-eval-002/q5_claims_enumeration.yaml`.

Only sentences from the verified-findings sections (everything before
'## Analyst Synthesis' / similar synthesis markers) with at least one [N]
citation are enumerated. Synthesis sections are hedged per the report
header and not audit-grade.
"""
from __future__ import annotations

import argparse
import json
import re
import sys
from pathlib import Path


CITATION_RE = re.compile(r"\[(\d+)\]")
SECTION_HEADER_RE = re.compile(r"^###\s+(.+?)\s*$", re.MULTILINE)
SYNTHESIS_MARKERS = (
    "## Analyst Synthesis",
    "## Synthesis",
    "*This section is analyst synthesis",
)


def split_into_sentences(text: str) -> list[str]:
    """Split paragraph text into sentences. Naive split on '. ' followed by
    capital/[. Preserves the trailing period.
    """
    # Normalize whitespace
    text = re.sub(r"\s+", " ", text.strip())
    if not text:
        return []
    # Sentence boundary: period/!/? followed by zero or more inline [N]
    # citation tokens, then whitespace, then a capital letter. Use a
    # capture group on the [N] tokens so they re-attach to the preceding
    # sentence rather than disappearing into the delimiter.
    parts = re.split(r"(?<=[.!?])((?:\[\d+\])*)\s+(?=[A-Z])", text)
    # parts alternates: [sentence, citations, sentence, citations, ..., sentence]
    out: list[str] = []
    i = 0
    while i < len(parts):
        sentence = parts[i]
        suffix = parts[i + 1] if i + 1 < len(parts) else ""
        out.append((sentence + suffix).strip())
        i += 2
    return [s for s in out if s]


def extract_verified_findings(report_md: str) -> dict[str, list[str]]:
    """Return {section_title: [sentences]} for verified-findings sections,
    stopping at the first synthesis marker.
    """
    # Truncate at first synthesis marker
    cut_at = len(report_md)
    for marker in SYNTHESIS_MARKERS:
        idx = report_md.find(marker)
        if idx != -1 and idx < cut_at:
            cut_at = idx
    verified_part = report_md[:cut_at]

    # Walk by ### section headers
    sections: dict[str, list[str]] = {}
    headers = list(SECTION_HEADER_RE.finditer(verified_part))
    if not headers:
        return sections
    for i, m in enumerate(headers):
        title = m.group(1).strip()
        start = m.end()
        end = headers[i + 1].start() if i + 1 < len(headers) else len(verified_part)
        body = verified_part[start:end].strip()
        sentences = split_into_sentences(body)
        # Only keep sentences that contain at least one [N] citation
        cited = [s for s in sentences if CITATION_RE.search(s)]
        if cited:
            sections[title] = cited
    return sections


def build_enumeration(report_dir: Path, prefix: str) -> dict:
    report_md = (report_dir / "report.md").read_text(encoding="utf-8")
    bibliography = json.loads((report_dir / "bibliography.json").read_text(encoding="utf-8"))
    pool = json.loads((report_dir / "evidence_pool.json").read_text(encoding="utf-8"))

    # num -> bibliography entry; evidence_id -> pool entry
    num_to_biblio = {b["num"]: b for b in bibliography}
    ev_to_pool = {e["evidence_id"]: e for e in pool}

    sections = extract_verified_findings(report_md)

    claims: list[dict] = []
    claim_idx = 0
    for section_title, sentences in sections.items():
        for sentence in sentences:
            claim_idx += 1
            claim_id = f"{prefix}-T1-{claim_idx:03d}"
            cited_nums = sorted({int(n) for n in CITATION_RE.findall(sentence)})
            cited_evidence: list[dict] = []
            for n in cited_nums:
                if n not in num_to_biblio:
                    continue
                biblio = num_to_biblio[n]
                ev_id = biblio["evidence_id"]
                pool_entry = ev_to_pool.get(ev_id, {})
                span_text = (pool_entry.get("direct_quote") or "").strip()
                # Cap span_text at ~600 chars for audit readability
                if len(span_text) > 600:
                    span_text = span_text[:600]
                cited_evidence.append({
                    "evidence_id": ev_id,
                    "bibliography_num": n,
                    "url": biblio.get("url", ""),
                    "tier": biblio.get("tier", "UNKNOWN"),
                    "span": "0-500",
                    "title": biblio.get("statement", "")[:160],
                    "span_text": span_text,
                })
            claims.append({
                "claim_id": claim_id,
                "section": section_title,
                "sentence": sentence,
                "cited_evidence": cited_evidence,
            })

    return {
        "schema_version": "tier1_v2",
        "report": str(report_dir / "report.md").replace("\\", "/"),
        "claim_count": len(claims),
        "claims": claims,
    }


def emit_yaml(doc: dict, out_path: Path) -> None:
    """Hand-rolled YAML emitter — avoids PyYAML dependency and produces the
    block-scalar form used by the iter-2 schema.
    """
    lines: list[str] = []
    lines.append(f"# Tier-1 v2 claim enumeration — {doc['report']}")
    lines.append(f"# Total claims: {doc['claim_count']}")
    lines.append("")
    lines.append(f"schema_version: {doc['schema_version']}")
    lines.append(f"report: {doc['report']}")
    lines.append("claims:")
    for c in doc["claims"]:
        lines.append(f"  - claim_id: {c['claim_id']}")
        lines.append(f"    section: {json.dumps(c['section'])}")
        lines.append(f"    sentence: {json.dumps(c['sentence'])}")
        lines.append("    cited_evidence:")
        for ev in c["cited_evidence"]:
            lines.append(f"      - evidence_id: {ev['evidence_id']}")
            lines.append(f"        bibliography_num: {ev['bibliography_num']}")
            lines.append(f"        url: {json.dumps(ev['url'])}")
            lines.append(f"        tier: {ev['tier']}")
            lines.append(f"        span: '{ev['span']}'")
            lines.append(f"        title: {json.dumps(ev['title'])}")
            lines.append("        span_text: |")
            for body_line in (ev["span_text"] or "").splitlines() or [""]:
                lines.append(f"          {body_line}")
        lines.append("    # tier1_to_fill: claim_type, materiality, citation_context_match, verdict, rationale, reviewer_confidence")
        lines.append("")
    out_path.write_text("\n".join(lines), encoding="utf-8")


def main() -> int:
    p = argparse.ArgumentParser()
    p.add_argument("--report-dir", required=True)
    p.add_argument("--prefix", required=True, help="Claim ID prefix, e.g. Q1")
    p.add_argument("--out", required=True)
    args = p.parse_args()

    report_dir = Path(args.report_dir)
    out_path = Path(args.out)
    out_path.parent.mkdir(parents=True, exist_ok=True)

    doc = build_enumeration(report_dir, args.prefix)
    emit_yaml(doc, out_path)
    print(f"Wrote {out_path} ({doc['claim_count']} claims)")
    return 0


if __name__ == "__main__":
    sys.exit(main())

| Report | Domain | Claims | VERIFIED | PARTIAL | UNSUPPORTED | %V |
|---|---|---:|---:|---:|---:|---:|
| Q1 | ai_sovereignty | 31 | 8 | 10 | 13 | 25.8% |
| Q2 | canada_us / CUSMA | 46 | 3 | 15 | 28 | 6.5% |
| Q3 | workforce / GenAI | 61 | 11 | 12 | 38 | 18.0% |
| Q4 | policy / housing | 51 | 12 | 9 | 30 | 23.5% |
| Q5 | policy / Pharmacare (PR #421 pilot) | 28 | 24 | 4 | 0 | 85.7% |
| **TOTAL** | | **217** | **58** | **50** | **109** | **26.7%** |

## Codex per-report context_match totals

| Report | yes | partial | no |
|---|---:|---:|---:|
| Q1 | 8 | 17 | 6 |
| Q2 | 7 | 27 | 12 |
| Q3 | 14 | 40 | 7 |
| Q4 | 12 | 31 | 8 |
| Q5 | n/a (v1 schema) | | |

## Headline finding

73% of audit-grade claims across Q1-Q4 are not VERIFIED against the cited span_text window. This breaks down approximately as:
- ~24% PARTIAL: span on-topic and broadly consistent, but the specific decimal/year/figure is not visible in the 500-char window POLARIS captured.
- ~49% UNSUPPORTED: cited span does not show the figure at all (per the 500-char window).

This is a sharp contrast with Q5 Pharmacare (85.7% VERIFIED), where the canonical source spans (Quebec RPAM, Bill C-64 PBO) contain headline figures in the first 500 chars.

## Limitation: span_text window scope

The audit operates on the `direct_quote` field (first ~500 chars of fetched source per evidence_id), per `tests/fixtures/` schema. The full source URL may contain the figure further down the page; the audit asserts "the captured span does not show the figure," not "the source URL definitively lacks the figure."

GH#398 (I-audit-001, completed) extended audit-bundle to include `resolved_report.md` + `bibliography.json` + `evidence_pool.json` so audits can read more than just the first 500 chars. The Tier-1 v2 schema here intentionally remains on the 500-char window since that is what POLARIS's strict_verify NLI gate evaluated when accepting the sentence into the report. A report-level claim that fails when audited against the exact same 500-char span POLARIS used is a real production quality concern — patient/policy decisions resting on that report would not have access to the wider context either.

## Q1-Q4 vs Q5 verdict-rate delta — three likely contributors

1. **Domain-source structure**: Q5's clinical-economic sources (Steve Morgan UBC pharmacare RPAM analysis; Quebec drug insurance PMC paper; PBO cost estimate) are written with the headline decimal in the introduction or abstract. Q1-Q4's policy/regulatory/labour sources scatter figures across multiple paragraphs.

2. **Pipeline emerging-policy threshold relaxation (GH#405 / PR #417)**: dropping T1 / T1+T2 / T1+T2+T3 minimums to 0 for emerging-policy domains let Q1-Q4 generate reports against sparser tier-1 corpora. The generator then selected what the corpus offered (often T4 secondary commentary or news), not primary T1-T3 evidence.

3. **Tier-balanced selector** picked 9/20 T4 + 4/20 UNKNOWN/T6/T7 in the post-fix Q5 sweep this turn — but Q5's underlying T4 sources DO contain the headline figures in the captured span. Q1-Q4 T4 sources tend not to.

## Recommended next steps (per Codex's path-quality criteria)

1. **Re-fetch full source content** for Q1-Q4 evidence rows where Codex returned UNSUPPORTED; extend `direct_quote` window from ~500 chars to full body (cap ~10K chars per evidence_id). Re-run Tier-1 audit on the same claims. This bounds the "audit-window vs source-content" gap.
2. **Investigate Q2 (CUSMA) specifically** — 6.5% V is the worst tier of any report and 93.5% PARTIAL/UNSUPPORTED suggests either (a) the CUSMA legal-text retrieval is mis-targeted (legal-section vs trade-flow facts), or (b) the generator is paraphrasing CUSMA Article references with figures from elsewhere.
3. **Claude independent Tier-1 pass on Q1-Q4** is INCOMPLETE per §-1.1 standard (only Q5 had paired Claude pass per PR #421). The Codex pass here covers 217/217 claims; Claude pass covers 28/217 (Q5 only). Reconciliation per §-1.1 requires both passes.

## Strictly NOT done in this aggregate (per §-1.1 banned-shortcut list)

- No metadata comparison framing ("ChatGPT has X, POLARIS has Y").
- No string-presence PASS/FAIL.
- No sample-based audit — 217/217 claims went through Codex line-by-line.
- No word-count or citation-count quality signals.

The Codex pass is the actual line-by-line work product. Each per-claim verdict is in `.codex/I-eval-003/codex_q{1..4}_batch_{N}_output.txt`. Cross-reference with `.codex/I-eval-003/q{1..4}_claims_enumeration.yaml` for the claim text + cited span.
